# 278\n
{London, British Library, Cotton Caligula A.ix, part 1, hand B, fols. 17va lines 1-4; 18vb line\n
7-26vb (foot); 27ra line 6 (yAT) -87vb (foot); 89rb line 4-194v (end): Layamon A}\n
C13b1\n
381 271 N\n
{N Worcs=}\n
{=Original transcript provided by JJS, corrected from microfilm by ML=}\n
{=Sample tagged: fols. 17va lines 1-4; 18vb-35va=}\n
{=Script - a very variable Textura semiquadrata=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{~f17va~}\n
{=As with hand A, I have tried to indicate capital letters as they\n
appear in the MS. These are not always the same as in the editions\n
of Madden or of Brook and Leslie (B&L). The rubrication used to indicate\n
capitals in the MS is not always clear on black and white microfilm and\n
I have tended to rely more on majuscule shape to assign the * indicating\n
capitalisation. There is bound to be some inconsistency, however, in the\n
final result. The line-breaks are those of the MS which is not written\n
in verse lines but as prose in two columns. Rhyme is irregular and when\n
it does occur is so often mixed with assonance or half-rhyme that I have\n
made no attempt to mark it except where there is a clear (pure-rhyme)\n
rhyming couplet marking the end of a section of the story or sense unit\n
Note that in the abbreviation for "that" - barred thorn - the bar touches\n
the ascender of thorn but does not bisect it. This figura does not seem\n
to be used, however, for an abbreviation for "there" and I have therefore\n
realised it as y~ not as yer. Contrast the hand of part 2 of this MS -\n
Owl and Nightingale - where the same figura, with the hook not touching\n
the ascender, is used for "that" and "there" and both have therefore been\n
realised as yer. Cf. also hand A of Layamon A similar to this. In fact\n
the main differences between hand A and hand B of Layamon are in the\n
generally greater regularity of hand A. It has both double and single\n
compartment A but seems never to have single compartment A with hooked\n
second element as is common in hand B. Hand B, however, is variable to\n
such an extent that most of hand A\'s characteristics are present in\n
his hand which makes separation of the stints a bit problematic.\n
There is also some difficulty in separating the usage in this hand of CC,\n
CT and TT because the scribe tends to use the same ligature for all three\n
I have not normalised as the editors do, but I transcribe the ligature\n
in each case as  cT whether it be for CT, TT or CC. The use of lower\n
case c in cT is so as to distinguish this purely orthographic usage from\n
genuine CT = [kt] and CT = ?[xt] in hands (including this one) where OE -ht\n
words have CT as a variant. These are transcribed CT. Cf. also the scribe of\n
the Caius Ancrene Riwle who uses a similar ligature for CT, CC and TT=}\n
{=B&L p70=}\n

before hand A resumes=}\n

$lay/vps12_+LE+STE $/vps12[V]_+STE\n
{=This reading is difficult. The MS has WORzLESTE which Madden and B&L\n
read as WORz LESTE though there is no gap in the MS. Madden translates\n
"how much worth esteemest thou me" taking LESTE to have the sense\n
"esteem" see MED lasten v. 1 sense 9c. This is, however, the only\n
example of "lasten" in this sense. It seems simpler therefore to take\n
this as a reflex of OE <le:ogan> "to lay" and translate: "how much\n
value do you place on me?" See MED leien (v.(1)) sense 9=}\n

{=So MS in main hand for KING. But revising hand changes to GAN\n
which would require the following past tense LIdDE to be read\n
as infinitive. B&L in fact emend it to LIdE. But the original\n

looks to me as if the original leg of K and the original following\n

!_HALKE\n
{=sic. Uncertain what is meant here. Madden suggests a mistake\n
for infinitive HALDE or HABBE. Perhaps more likely an error for HAWE\n
since K in this hand is very similar to the second element of W. This\n
might then be for infinitive of "have" or for "own" (< OE a:gan), except\n
that this scribe habitually has HABBE(N) for the former and AzEN for the\n
latter. B&L accept the emendation to HALDE which makes sense and would\n
match the Otho version. An otherwise unknown verb based on OE heal[o]c\n
"hollow" "hiding place" is formally possible but not plausible in\n
context. Cf also  MED halchen? "tie up" "fasten", apparently related to\n
OE halsian and appearing several times in Gawain in relation to knightly\n
caparison. If it were a unpalatalised form of an OE *h(e)alc(i)an with\n
one or other of the meanings given in MED for halchen, it could perhaps\n
mean  either "greet" or "equip", especially as the knights are then said\n
to be "hung with mantles"=}\n
$in{p}/pr_IN\n
$/P13GM_IS\n
$house/n<pr_HEOSE\n

$/P13GM_HIS-S\n
{=MS HIS S. This seems not to be an instance of Orm-like doubling of\n
syllable final consonant because of the clear space. Cf. SHULDEN-N\n
below. As well as repeating the last letter of a word at a line end, the\n
scribe also has a tendency to start a word with a single letter at the\n
line end and then write the whole word on the next line. These may be\n
idiosyncratic line filling ploys. Most scribes like to have the columns\n
full except where a new section begins. This scribe also sometimes\n
capitalises or makes a the final letter of a line exaggeratedly big to\n
solve the problem of line end gaps. Later in the manuscript he also\n
adopts a habit for a while of using 3 or more horizontal lines to fill\n
a small space. It is noticeable that hand A while writing much more\n
neatly and regularly in general makes no special attempt to justify\n
the right margins of this columns. They are distinctly wavy. When\n
hand B takes over with his much more irregular script he also doesn\'t\n
justify very strictly. But from fol. 23r (this folio) there seems to\n
begin an effort to make the two blocks of text per page justify right\n
as well as left. It isn\'t completely successful, and later on he has\n
phases where he seems not to worry about it so much, but for long\n
stretches the general effect is much more of equal text blocks.\n
The scribe uses a number of different ploys, repeating letters being\n
the most unusual (to me). He sometimes puts two puncta instead of just\n
one and he also enlarges final letters of the line either by capitalising\n
(marked with *) or by pulling out cross bars to greater than normal\n
length or by simply enlarging the letter. Sometimes he leaves a gap\n
between the penultimate and ultimate letter, occasionally the gap is\n
filled by two horizontal strokes=}\n
{\\}\n

$wael/n<pr_WALLE\n
{=Madden suggests emendation to WALDE "wold". Otho has IN yIS FELDE which\n
looks like a paraphrase. "Wall" would be possible, formally, but makes\n
little sense contextually. The soldiers are taking their dead enemies\'\n
weapons in order to deceive the other side into thinking they are\n
friends. It seems best therefore to take the word as wael a collective\n
singular meaning "the slain". Doubling consonants after short vowels is\n
something this scribe does quite often. This would successfully\n
account also for the preposition BI which would be odd with Madden\'s\n
emendation=}\n

$as/cj_AL-SE\n
$cy:f/npl_CUU+ES $/pln_+ES\n
{=MS could be read CUNES or CUUES. Madden and B&L assume CUNES\n
and emend to TUNES. But "as if towns burned" bears no relation\n
to a stormy sea and the simile cannot be right. I see no reason\n
why it can\'t be CUUE from OE cy:f "vat" or "tub" as used in brewing\n
or dyeing. See MED kive and OED keeve. "Burn" is used in the sense\n
"heat up" "boil", see MED brennen 4d. Then the simile is what one\n
would expect - the waves are rolling (MED rennen 9c) like a boiling\n
vat. Madden points out that almost the same phrase occurs on fol. 79va,\n
but there the revising hand has altered the relevant word to TUNES by\n
supplying TU over a now illegible erasure. The original probably had \n
some form of OE cy:f here too - if so it was presumably not understood\n
by the reviser. See further Laing (2001: 100-108=}\n
$there/av_yER\n

$rope/npl_*RAP+ES $/pln_+ES\n

$le:ofli:ce/av_LEOF+LICHE $-ly/xs-av_+LICHE\n
$e:ode?/vpt23_OUDEN\n
{=Or read ONDEN. The text may be corrupt here. It is not clear what is\n
meant. Unless this is the verb (3rd pl pret required) there appears\n
to be a verb missing but with no metrical space to accommodate it\n
if it is missing. Even Madden\'s and B&L\'s radical emendation of ONDEN\n
to ON CNEOWEN doesn\'t solve this problem unless you wait for FULLEN\n
in the next line. But the punctus after ONDEN seems to suggest a break\n
in the sense. Otho\'s version - AND FULLEN A CNOWE AT yAN KINGES FOTE\n
is evidently a paraphrase. If the reading is ONDEN, formally dat sg. or\n
pl of "hand" seems the easiest reading, but would still need a verb\n
meaning "stretched forth", or some such, suggesting greeting. The metre\n
would require following FORN which is otherwise unexceptional to be a\n
corruption of such a verb and it\'s hard to see what it could be. But\n
the word can equally well, palaeographaically, be read as OUDEN. This\n
could in this scribe\'s system by read as the pret pl of "yede" with OU\n
for EO. His usual forms are EODE(N), but cf. TOUNEN for TEONEN (fol.\n
34vb line 7 B&L line 2921 (also LEO and LOU both for "lo"). A\n
conjectural form in OUDEN is not completely implausible. The meaning\n
would be OK if a little feeble "they lovingly went before the king".\n
Just as plausible formally in this scribe\'s system is the idea that it\n
may be 3rd pers pl pret of unnan with OU for long u: and D for edh.\n
The sense required seems to be something like "greet" rather than\n
"grant". But intransitively the verb can mean (see MED unnen 3(a))\n
"regard with favour", "wish someone well". This would fit OK with\n
LEOFLICHE. They are besides "granting" to the kings the whole of Rome\n
in the next sentence. The construction remains awkward, however, and\n
my best guess at present is the pret pl of "yede"=}\n

{*sj context*}\n
{=DEN crossed through ?by revising hand. Madden and B&L read as SLaeN\n
and read the crossed through letters as DAN. But the middle letter\n
looks like E to me. Although Otho has AND SLEAN HEOM ALLE BI-TWINE,\n
the phrase "and slay/strike between them" doesn\'t read right to me. Why not\n
assume that hand B has it right and it is a form of MED slenden v.\n
(origin unknown, but cf. dialect "slent"). It could be translated "and\n
sliced between them". Since the idea is evidently to cut them off and\n
surround them with two different armies, this seems to make sense=}\n
